Thus, the guerrillas were unable to communicate and be resupplied, leaving them isolated and stranded.<sup id="cite_ref-250"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup>

In addition, Guevara's known preference for confrontation rather than compromise, which had previously surfaced during his guerrilla warfare campaign in Cuba, contributed to his inability to develop successful working relationships with local rebel leaders in Bolivia, just as it had in the Congo.<sup id="cite_ref-251"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> This tendency had existed in Cuba, but had been kept in check by the timely interventions and guidance of Fidel Castro.<sup id="cite_ref-252"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup>

The result was that Guevara was unable to attract inhabitants of the local area to join his militia during the eleven months he attempted recruitment. Many of the inhabitants willingly informed the Bolivian authorities and military about the guerrillas and their movements in the area. Near the end of the Bolivian venture, Guevara wrote in his diary: "Talking to these peasants is like talking to statues. They do not give us any help. Worse still, many of them are turning into informants."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright200086_253-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright200086-253">[253]</a></sup>

[Félix Rodríguez](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_Rodr%C3%ADguez_(soldier) "Félix Rodríguez (soldier)"), a [Cuban exile](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuban_exile "Cuban exile") turned CIA Special Activities Division operative, advised Bolivian troops during the hunt for Guevara in Bolivia.<sup id="cite_ref-254"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> In addition, the 2007 documentary _[My Enemy's Enemy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/My_Enemy%27s_Enemy "My Enemy's Enemy")_ alleges that [Nazi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazism "Nazism") war criminal [Klaus Barbie](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Klaus_Barbie#Che_Guevara "Klaus Barbie") advised and possibly helped the CIA orchestrate Guevara's eventual capture.<sup id="cite_ref-ObserverChe_255-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-ObserverChe-255">[255]</a></sup>

### Capture

On 7 October 1967, an informant apprised the Bolivian Special Forces of the location of Guevara's guerrilla encampment in the Yuro ravine.<sup id="cite_ref-256"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> On the morning of 8 October, they encircled the area with two companies numbering 180 soldiers and advanced into the ravine triggering a battle where Guevara was wounded and taken prisoner while leading a detachment with [Simeon Cuba Sarabia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simeon_Cuba_Sarabia "Simeon Cuba Sarabia").<sup id="cite_ref-257"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> Che's biographer [Jon Lee Anderson](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jon_Lee_Anderson "Jon Lee Anderson") reports Bolivian Sergeant Bernardino Huanca's account: that as the Bolivian Rangers approached, a twice-wounded Guevara, his gun rendered useless, threw up his arms in surrender and shouted to the soldiers: "Do not shoot! I am Che Guevara and I am worth more to you alive than dead."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson1997733_258-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson1997733-258">[258]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ernesto_%22Che%22_Guevara_captured.jpg)

Guevara captured. Photo taken a half-hour before his execution. Félix Rodríguez is on the left.

Guevara was tied up and taken to a dilapidated mud schoolhouse in the nearby village of [La Higuera](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/La_Higuera "La Higuera") on the evening of 8 October. For the next half-day, Guevara refused to be interrogated by Bolivian officers and only spoke quietly to Bolivian soldiers. One of those Bolivian soldiers, a helicopter pilot named Jaime Nino de Guzman, describes Che as looking "dreadful". According to Guzman, Guevara was shot through the right calf, his hair was matted with dirt, his clothes were shredded, and his feet were covered in rough leather sheaths. Despite his haggard appearance, he recounts that "Che held his head high, looked everyone straight in the eyes and asked only for something to smoke." De Guzman states that he "took pity" and gave him a small bag of tobacco for his pipe, and that Guevara then smiled and thanked him.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Man_Who_Buried_Che_259-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-The_Man_Who_Buried_Che-259">[259]</a></sup> Later on the night of 8 October, Guevara—despite having his hands tied—kicked a Bolivian army officer, named Captain Espinosa, against a wall after the officer entered the schoolhouse and tried to snatch Guevara's pipe from his mouth as a souvenir while he was still smoking it.<sup id="cite_ref-Michèle_260-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Mich%C3%A8le-260">[260]</a></sup> In another instance of defiance, Guevara spat in the face of Bolivian Rear Admiral Horacio Ugarteche, who attempted to question Guevara a few hours before his execution.<sup id="cite_ref-Michèle_260-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Mich%C3%A8le-260">[260]</a></sup>

The following morning on 9 October, Guevara asked to see the school teacher of the village, a 22-year-old woman named Julia Cortez. She later stated that she found Guevara to be an "agreeable looking man with a soft and ironic glance" and that during their conversation she found herself "unable to look him in the eye" because his "gaze was unbearable, piercing, and so tranquil".<sup id="cite_ref-Michèle_260-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Mich%C3%A8le-260">[260]</a></sup> During their short conversation, Guevara pointed out to Cortez the poor condition of the schoolhouse, stating that it was "anti-[pedagogical](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pedagogy "Pedagogy")" to expect campesino students to be educated there, while "government officials drive [Mercedes](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercedes_(marque) "Mercedes (marque)") cars"; Guevara said "that's what we are fighting against".<sup id="cite_ref-Michèle_260-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Mich%C3%A8le-260">[260]</a></sup>

### Execution

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Vallegrande_location.png)

Location of [Vallegrande](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vallegrande "Vallegrande") in Bolivia

Later on the morning of 9 October, Bolivian President [René Barrientos](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Barrientos "René Barrientos") ordered that Guevara be killed. The order was relayed to the unit holding Guevara by Félix Rodríguez reportedly despite the United States government's desire that Guevara be taken to Panama for further interrogation.<sup id="cite_ref-261"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> The executioner who volunteered to kill Guevara was [Mario Terán](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mario_Ter%C3%A1n "Mario Terán"), a 27-year-old sergeant in the Bolivian army who while [half-drunk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alcohol_intoxication "Alcohol intoxication") requested to shoot Guevara because three of his friends from B Company, all with the same first name of "Mario", had been killed in a firefight several days earlier with Guevara's band of guerrillas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaibo1999267_9-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaibo1999267-9">[9]</a></sup> To make the bullet wounds appear consistent with the story that the Bolivian government planned to release to the public, Félix Rodríguez ordered Terán not to shoot Guevara in the head, but to aim carefully to make it appear that Guevara had been killed in action during a clash with the Bolivian army.<sup id="cite_ref-262"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> [Gary Prado Salmón](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gary_Prado_Salm%C3%B3n "Gary Prado Salmón"), the Bolivian captain in command of the army company that captured Guevara, said that the reasons Barrientos ordered the immediate execution of Guevara were so there could be no possibility for Guevara to escape from prison, and also so there could be no drama of a public trial where adverse publicity might happen.<sup id="cite_ref-263"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup>

About 30 minutes before Guevara was killed, Félix Rodríguez attempted to question him about the whereabouts of other guerrilla fighters who were currently at large, but Guevara continued to remain silent. Rodríguez, assisted by a few Bolivian soldiers, helped Guevara to his feet and took him outside the hut to parade him before other Bolivian soldiers where he posed with Guevara for a [photo opportunity](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photo_opportunity "Photo opportunity") where one soldier took a photograph of Rodríguez and other soldiers standing alongside Guevara. Afterwards, Rodríguez told Guevara that he was going to be executed. A little later, Guevara was asked by one of the Bolivian soldiers guarding him if he was thinking about his own immortality. "No" he replied, "I'm thinking about the immortality of the revolution".<sup id="cite_ref-264"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> A few minutes later, Sergeant Terán entered the hut to shoot him, whereupon Guevara reportedly stood up and spoke to Terán what were his last words: "I know you've come to kill me. Shoot, coward! You are only going to kill a man!" Terán hesitated, then pointed his self-loading [M2 carbine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/M1_carbine#Carbine,_Cal_0,_M2 "M1 carbine")<sup id="cite_ref-265"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> at Guevara and opened fire, hitting him in the arms and legs.<sup id="cite_ref-anderson739_266-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-anderson739-266">[266]</a></sup> Then, as Guevara writhed on the ground, apparently biting one of his wrists to avoid crying out, Terán fired another burst, fatally wounding him in the chest. Guevara was pronounced dead at 1:10 pm local time according to Rodríguez.<sup id="cite_ref-anderson739_266-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-anderson739-266">[266]</a></sup> In all, Guevara was shot nine times by Terán. This included five times in his legs, once in the right shoulder and arm, and once in the chest and throat.<sup id="cite_ref-Michèle_260-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Mich%C3%A8le-260">[260]</a></sup>

Months earlier, during his last public declaration to the [Tricontinental Conference](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tricontinental_Conference_(1966) "Tricontinental Conference (1966)"),<sup id="cite_ref-MessTricont1967_210-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-MessTricont1967-210">[210]</a></sup> Guevara had written his own [epitaph](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epitaph "Epitaph"), stating: "Wherever death may surprise us, let it be welcome, provided that this our battle cry may have reached some receptive ear and another hand may be extended to wield our weapons."<sup id="cite_ref-267"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup>

### Aftermath

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:FreddyAlbertoChe.jpg)

The day after his execution on 10 October 1967, Guevara's corpse was displayed to the news media in the laundry house of the Vallegrande hospital (photo by [Freddy Alborta](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Freddy_Alborta&action=edit&redlink=1 "Freddy Alborta (page does not exist)") \[[es](https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freddy_Alborta "es:Freddy Alborta")\])  
         **[](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Camera-photo.svg)    [Face](https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Image:CheExec9B.jpg "commons:Image:CheExec9B.jpg")     [Side angle](https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Image:CheExec19.jpg "commons:Image:CheExec19.jpg")    [Shoes](https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Image:Exec22.gif "commons:Image:Exec22.gif")**

After his execution, Guevara's body was lashed to the landing skids of a helicopter and flown to nearby [Vallegrande](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vallegrande "Vallegrande"), where photographs were taken of him lying on a concrete slab in the laundry room of the Nuestra Señora de Malta.<sup id="cite_ref-268"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup> Several witnesses were called to confirm his identity, key amongst them the British journalist [Richard Gott](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Gott "Richard Gott"), the only witness to have met Guevara when he was alive. Put on display, as hundreds of local residents filed past the body, Guevara's corpse was considered by many to represent a "Christ-like" visage, with some even surreptitiously clipping locks of his hair as divine relics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECasey2009179_269-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTECasey2009179-269">[269]</a></sup> Such comparisons were further extended when English art critic [John Berger](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Berger "John Berger"), two weeks later upon seeing the post-mortem photographs, observed that they resembled two famous paintings: [Rembrandt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rembrandt "Rembrandt")'s _[The Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr._Nicolaes_Tulp "The Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp")_ and [Andrea Mantegna](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrea_Mantegna "Andrea Mantegna")'s _[Lamentation over the Dead Christ](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lamentation_over_the_Dead_Christ_(Mantegna) "Lamentation over the Dead Christ (Mantegna)")_.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECasey2009183_270-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTECasey2009183-270">[270]</a></sup> There were also four correspondents present when Guevara's body arrived in Vallegrande, including [Björn Kumm](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bj%C3%B6rn_Kumm "Björn Kumm") of the Swedish _[Aftonbladet](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aftonbladet "Aftonbladet")_, who described the scene in an 11 November 1967, exclusive for _[The New Republic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_New_Republic "The New Republic")_.<sup id="cite_ref-271"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup>

A declassified memorandum dated 11 October 1967 to [United States President](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_the_United_States "President of the United States") [Lyndon B. Johnson](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson "Lyndon B. Johnson") from his [National Security Advisor](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Security_Advisor_(United_States) "National Security Advisor (United States)") [Walt Rostow](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walt_Rostow "Walt Rostow"), called the decision to kill Guevara "stupid" but "understandable from a Bolivian standpoint".<sup id="cite_ref-272"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup> After the execution, Rodríguez took several of Guevara's personal items, including a watch which he continued to wear many years later, often showing them to reporters during the ensuing years.<sup id="cite_ref-273"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> Today, some of these belongings, including his flashlight, are on display at the CIA.<sup id="cite_ref-274"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> After a military doctor [dismembered](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dismemberment "Dismemberment") his hands, Bolivian army officers transferred Guevara's body to an undisclosed location and refused to reveal whether his remains had been buried or cremated. The hands were sent to Buenos Aires for fingerprint identification. They were later sent to Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-275"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup>

On 15 October in Havana, [Fidel Castro](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fidel_Castro "Fidel Castro") publicly acknowledged that Guevara was dead and proclaimed three days of public mourning throughout Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson1997740_276-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson1997740-276">[276]</a></sup> On 18 October, Castro addressed a crowd of one million mourners in Havana's [Plaza de la Revolución](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plaza_de_la_Revoluci%C3%B3n "Plaza de la Revolución") and spoke about Guevara's character as a revolutionary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson1997741_277-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson1997741-277">[277]</a></sup> Fidel Castro closed his impassioned eulogy thus:

> If we wish to express what we want the men of future generations to be, we must say: Let them be like Che! If we wish to say how we want our children to be educated, we must say without hesitation: We want them to be educated in Che's spirit! If we want the model of a man, who does not belong to our times but to the future, I say from the depths of my heart that such a model, without a single stain on his conduct, without a single stain on his action, is Che!<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKellner1989101_278-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKellner1989101-278">[278]</a></sup>

Also removed when Guevara was captured were his 30,000-word, hand-written diary, a collection of his personal poetry, and a short story he had authored about a young communist guerrilla who learns to overcome his fears.<sup id="cite_ref-279"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup> His diary documented events of the guerrilla campaign in Bolivia,<sup id="cite_ref-280"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> with the first entry on 7 November 1966, shortly after his arrival at the farm in Ñancahuazú, and the last dated 7 October 1967, the day before his capture. The diary tells how the guerrillas were forced to begin operations prematurely because of discovery by the Bolivian Army, explains Guevara's decision to divide the column into two units that were subsequently unable to re-establish contact, and describes their overall unsuccessful venture. It also records the rift between Guevara and the Communist Party of Bolivia that resulted in Guevara having significantly fewer soldiers than originally expected, and shows that Guevara had a great deal of difficulty recruiting from the local populace, partly because the guerrilla group had learned [Quechua](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quechuan_languages "Quechuan languages"), unaware that the local language was actually a [Tupi–Guarani](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tupi%E2%80%93Guarani_languages "Tupi–Guarani languages") language.<sup id="cite_ref-281"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> As the campaign drew to an unexpected close, Guevara became increasingly ill. He endured ever-worsening bouts of asthma, and most of his last offensives were carried out in an attempt to obtain medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-282"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup> The Bolivian diary was quickly and crudely translated by _[Ramparts](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ramparts_(magazine) "Ramparts (magazine)")_ magazine and circulated around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-283"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> There are at least four additional diaries in existence—those of Israel Reyes Zayas (Alias "Braulio"), Harry Villegas Tamayo (["Pombo"](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harry_Villegas "Harry Villegas")), Eliseo Reyes Rodriguez ("Rolando")<sup id="cite_ref-#refSelvage1985|Selvage_1985_244-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-#refSelvage1985%7CSelvage_1985-244">[244]</a></sup> and Dariel Alarcón Ramírez ("Benigno")<sup id="cite_ref-284"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup>—each of which reveals additional aspects of the events.

French [intellectual](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intellectual "Intellectual") [Régis Debray](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/R%C3%A9gis_Debray "Régis Debray"), who was captured in April 1967 while with Guevara in Bolivia, gave an interview from prison in August 1968, in which he enlarged on the circumstances of Guevara's capture. Debray, who had lived with Guevara's band of guerrillas for a short time, said that in his view they were "victims of the forest" and thus "eaten by the jungle".<sup id="cite_ref-Nadle_285-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Nadle-285">[285]</a></sup> Debray described a destitute situation where Guevara's men suffered malnutrition, lack of water, absence of shoes, and only possessed six blankets for 22 men. Debray recounts that Guevara and the others had been suffering an "illness" which caused their hands and feet to swell into "mounds of flesh" to the point where you could not discern the fingers on their hands. Debray described Guevara as "optimistic about the future of Latin America" despite the futile situation, and remarked that Guevara was "resigned to die in the knowledge that his death would be a sort of renaissance", noting that Guevara perceived death "as a promise of rebirth" and "ritual of renewal".<sup id="cite_ref-Nadle_285-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Nadle-285">[285]</a></sup>

## Legacy

### Political left

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Che_Guevara_-_flag.jpg)

A stylized graphic of Guevara's face on a flag above the words "El Che Vive!" (Che Lives!)

[Radical left](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Far-left_politics "Far-left politics") wing activists responded to Guevara's apparent indifference to rewards and glory, and concurred with Guevara's sanctioning of violence as a necessity to instill [socialist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialism_(Marxism) "Socialism (Marxism)") ideals.<sup id="cite_ref-286"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup> Even in the United States, the government which Guevara so vigorously denounced, students began to emulate his style of dress, donning military fatigues, [berets](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beret "Beret"), and growing their hair and beards to show that they too were opponents of US foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-287"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup> For instance, the [Black Panthers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Panthers "Black Panthers") began to style themselves "Che-type" while adopting his trademark black [beret](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beret "Beret"), while Arab guerrillas began to name combat operations in his honor.<sup id="cite_ref-Embalm_288-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Embalm-288">[288]</a></sup> Addressing the wide-ranging flexibility of his legacy, Trisha Ziff, director of the 2008 documentary _[Chevolution](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevolution "Chevolution")_, has remarked that "Che Guevara's significance in modern times is less about the man and his specific history, and more about the ideals of creating a better society."<sup id="cite_ref-289"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup> In a similar vein, the Chilean writer [Ariel Dorfman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ariel_Dorfman "Ariel Dorfman") has suggested Guevara's enduring appeal might be because "to those who will never follow in his footsteps, submerged as they are in a world of cynicism, self-interest and frantic consumption, nothing could be more vicariously gratifying than Che's disdain for material comfort and everyday desires."<sup id="cite_ref-BBC02_290-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-BBC02-290">[290]</a></sup>

To a certain extent, the belief of Guevara's of a metaphorical resurrection after death came true. After pictures of the dead Guevara began being circulated and the circumstances of his death were being debated, Che's legend began to spread. Demonstrations in protest against his "assassination" occurred throughout the world, and articles, tributes, and poems were written about his life and death.<sup id="cite_ref-291"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup> Rallies in support of Guevara were held from "Mexico to [Santiago](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Santiago "Santiago"), [Algiers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algiers "Algiers") to Angola, and [Cairo](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cairo "Cairo") to [Calcutta](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kolkata "Kolkata")".<sup id="cite_ref-292"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup> The population of [Budapest](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Budapest "Budapest") and [Prague](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prague "Prague") lit candles to honor Guevara's passing; and the picture of a smiling Che appeared in London and Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-293"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-293">[293]</a></sup> When a few months later riots broke out in [Berlin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berlin "Berlin"), [France](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/May_68 "May 68"), and [Chicago](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1968_Democratic_National_Convention_protests "1968 Democratic National Convention protests"), and the unrest spread to the American college campuses, young men and women wore Che Guevara T-shirts and carried his pictures during their protest marches. In the view of military historian [Erik Durschmied](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erik_Durschmied "Erik Durschmied"): "In those [heady months of 1968](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protests_of_1968 "Protests of 1968"), Che Guevara was not dead. He was very much alive."<sup id="cite_ref-294"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup>

### Retrieval of remains

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Che_Guevara_-_Grab_in_Santa_Clara,_Kuba.jpg)

[Che Guevara's Monument and Mausoleum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara_Mausoleum "Che Guevara Mausoleum") in [Santa Clara](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Santa_Clara,_Cuba "Santa Clara, Cuba"), Cuba

In late 1995, the retired [Bolivian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolivia "Bolivia") General Mario Vargas revealed to [Jon Lee Anderson](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jon_Lee_Anderson "Jon Lee Anderson"), author of _Che Guevara: A Revolutionary Life_, that Guevara's corpse lay near a [Vallegrande](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vallegrande "Vallegrande") airstrip. The result was a multi-national search for the remains, which lasted more than a year. In July 1997, a team of Cuban geologists and Argentine [forensic anthropologists](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forensic_anthropology "Forensic anthropology") discovered the remnants of seven bodies in two mass graves, including one man without hands (as Guevara would have been). Bolivian government officials with the Ministry of Interior later identified the body as Guevara when the excavated teeth "perfectly matched" a plaster mold of Che's teeth made in Cuba prior to his Congolese expedition. The "clincher" then arrived when Argentine forensic anthropologist Alejandro Inchaurregui inspected the inside hidden pocket of a blue jacket dug up next to the handless cadaver and found a small bag of pipe tobacco. Nino de Guzman, the Bolivian helicopter pilot who had given Che a small bag of tobacco, later remarked that he "had serious doubts" at first and "thought the Cubans would just find any old bones and call it Che"; but "after hearing about the tobacco pouch, I have no doubts."<sup id="cite_ref-The_Man_Who_Buried_Che_259-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-The_Man_Who_Buried_Che-259">[259]</a></sup> On 17 October 1997 (30 years and 8 days after Guevara's death), Guevara's remains, with those of six of his fellow combatants, were laid to rest with military honors in a specially built [mausoleum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara_Mausoleum "Che Guevara Mausoleum") in the Cuban city of [Santa Clara](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Santa_Clara,_Cuba "Santa Clara, Cuba"), where he had commanded over the [decisive military victory](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Santa_Clara "Battle of Santa Clara") of the [Cuban Revolution](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuban_Revolution "Cuban Revolution").<sup id="cite_ref-295"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup>

In July 2008, the Bolivian government of [Evo Morales](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evo_Morales "Evo Morales") unveiled Guevara's formerly-sealed diaries composed in two frayed notebooks, along with a logbook and several black-and-white photographs. At this event Bolivia's vice-minister of culture, [Pablo Groux](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pablo_Groux "Pablo Groux"), expressed that there were plans to publish photographs of every handwritten page later in the year.<sup id="cite_ref-296"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup> Meanwhile, in August 2009 anthropologists working for Bolivia's Justice Ministry discovered and unearthed the bodies of five of Guevara's fellow guerrillas near the Bolivian town of [Teoponte](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teoponte "Teoponte").<sup id="cite_ref-297"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup>

> The discovery of Che's remains metonymically activated a series of interlinked associations—rebel, martyr, rogue figure from a picaresque adventure, savior, renegade, extremist—in which there was no fixed divide among them. The current court of opinion places Che on a continuum that teeters between viewing him as a misguided rebel, a coruscatingly brilliant guerrilla philosopher, a poet-warrior jousting at windmills, a brazen warrior who threw down the gauntlet to the bourgeoisie, the object of fervent paeans to his sainthood, or a mass murderer clothed in the guise of an avenging angel whose every action is imbricated in violence—the archetypal Fanatical Terrorist.
> 
> — Dr. [Peter McLaren](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_McLaren "Peter McLaren"), author of _Che Guevara, Paulo Freire, and the Pedagogy of Revolution_<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcLaren20007_298-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLaren20007-298">[298]</a></sup>

### Biographical debate

Guevara's life and legacy remain contentious. The perceived contradictions of his ethos at various points in his life have created a complex character of duality, one who was "able to wield the pen and submachine gun with equal skill", while prophesying that "the most important revolutionary ambition was to see man liberated from [his alienation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_alienation "Marx's theory of alienation")".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELöwy19737,_33_299-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEL%C3%B6wy19737,_33-299">[299]</a></sup> Guevara's paradoxical standing is further complicated by his array of seemingly diametrically opposed qualities. A [secular humanist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secular_humanism "Secular humanism") and sympathetic practitioner of medicine who did not hesitate to shoot his enemies, a celebrated [internationalist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proletarian_internationalism "Proletarian internationalism") leader who advocated violence to enforce a [utopian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utopia "Utopia") philosophy of the [collective good](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_good_(economics) "Public good (economics)"), an [idealistic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Idealism "Idealism") [intellectual](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intellectual "Intellectual") who loved literature but refused to allow dissent, an [anti-imperialist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-imperialism "Anti-imperialism") [Marxist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marxism "Marxism") [insurgent](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insurgency "Insurgency") who was radically willing to forge a poverty-less new world on the apocalyptic ashes of the old one, and finally, an outspoken [anti-capitalist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-capitalism "Anti-capitalism") whose image has been [commoditized](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commodification "Commodification"). Che's history continues to be rewritten and re-imagined.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELöwy19737,_9,_15,_25,_75,_106_300-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEL%C3%B6wy19737,_9,_15,_25,_75,_106-300">[300]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Löwy_301-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-L%C3%B6wy-301">[301]</a></sup> Moreover, [sociologist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sociology "Sociology") [Michael Löwy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_L%C3%B6wy "Michael Löwy") contends that the many facets of Guevara's life (i.e. doctor and economist, revolutionary and banker, military theoretician and ambassador, deep thinker and political agitator) illuminated the rise of the "Che myth", allowing him to be invariably crystallized in his many [metanarrative](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metanarrative "Metanarrative") roles as a "Red [Robin Hood](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robin_Hood "Robin Hood"), [Don Quixote](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Don_Quixote "Don Quixote") of communism, new [Garibaldi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giuseppe_Garibaldi "Giuseppe Garibaldi"), Marxist [Saint Just](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Antoine_de_Saint-Just "Louis Antoine de Saint-Just"), [Cid Campeador](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_Cid "El Cid") of [the Wretched of the Earth](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Wretched_of_the_Earth "The Wretched of the Earth"), [Sir Galahad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galahad "Galahad") of the beggars ... and [Bolshevik](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolsheviks "Bolsheviks") devil who haunts the dreams of the rich, kindling braziers of subversion all over the world".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELöwy19737_302-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEL%C3%B6wy19737-302">[302]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Chile_quema_libros_1973.JPG)

The burning of a painting containing Che's face, following the [1973 coup](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1973_Chilean_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat "1973 Chilean coup d'état") that installed the [Pinochet regime](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_government_of_Chile_(1973%E2%80%931990) "Military government of Chile (1973–1990)") in Chile

As such, various notable individuals have lauded Guevara; for example, [Nelson Mandela](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nelson_Mandela "Nelson Mandela") referred to him as "an inspiration for every human being who loves freedom",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuevara2009II_303-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuevara2009II-303">[303]</a></sup> while [Jean-Paul Sartre](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre "Jean-Paul Sartre") described him as "not only an intellectual but also the most complete human being of our age".<sup id="cite_ref-304"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup> Others who have expressed their admiration include authors [Graham Greene](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graham_Greene "Graham Greene"), who remarked that Guevara "represented the idea of gallantry, chivalry, and adventure",<sup id="cite_ref-305"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup> and [Susan Sontag](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Susan_Sontag "Susan Sontag"), who supposed that "\[Che's\] goal was nothing less than the cause of humanity itself."<sup id="cite_ref-306"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup> In the [Pan-African](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-Africanism "Pan-Africanism") community philosopher [Frantz Fanon](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frantz_Fanon "Frantz Fanon") professed Guevara to be "the world symbol of the possibilities of one man",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcLaren20003_307-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLaren20003-307">[307]</a></sup> while [Black Power](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_power "Black power") leader [Stokely Carmichael](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stokely_Carmichael "Stokely Carmichael") eulogized that "Che Guevara is not dead, his ideas are with us."<sup id="cite_ref-308"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup> Praise has been reflected throughout the political spectrum, with [libertarian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libertarianism "Libertarianism") theorist [Murray Rothbard](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Murray_Rothbard "Murray Rothbard") extolling Guevara as a "heroic figure" who "more than any man of our epoch or even of our century, was the living embodiment of the principle of revolution",<sup id="cite_ref-309"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup> while journalist [Christopher Hitchens](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens "Christopher Hitchens") reminisced that "\[Che's\] death meant a lot to me and countless like me at the time, he was a role model, albeit an impossible one for us [bourgeois](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bourgeoisie "Bourgeoisie") [romantics](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanticism "Romanticism") insofar as he went and did what revolutionaries were meant to do—fought and died for his beliefs."<sup id="cite_ref-myth_310-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-myth-310">[310]</a></sup> Former CIA employee [Philip Agee](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philip_Agee "Philip Agee") said "There was no person more feared by the company (CIA) than Che Guevara because he had the capacity and charisma necessary to direct the struggle against the political repression of the traditional hierarchies in power in the countries of Latin America".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuevara2009II_303-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuevara2009II-303">[303]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:4CheFaces.jpg)

Author Michael Casey notes how [Che's image](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guerrillero_Heroico "Guerrillero Heroico") has become a logo as recognizable as the [Nike Swoosh](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swoosh "Swoosh") or [McDonald's Golden Arches](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_Arches "Golden Arches").<sup id="cite_ref-Michiko_211-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Michiko-211">[211]</a></sup>

Conversely, Jacobo Machover, an exiled opposition author, dismisses all praise of Guevara and portrays him as a callous executioner.<sup id="cite_ref-Machover_311-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Machover-311">[311]</a></sup> Exiled former Cuban prisoners have expressed similar opinions, among them [Armando Valladares](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armando_Valladares "Armando Valladares"), who declared Guevara "a man full of hatred" who executed dozens without trial,<sup id="cite_ref-312"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-312">[312]</a></sup> and [Carlos Alberto Montaner](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carlos_Alberto_Montaner "Carlos Alberto Montaner"), who asserted that Guevara possessed "a [Robespierre](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maximilien_Robespierre "Maximilien Robespierre") mentality", wherein cruelty against the revolution's enemies was a virtue.<sup id="cite_ref-313"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-313">[313]</a></sup> [Álvaro Vargas Llosa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_Vargas_Llosa "Álvaro Vargas Llosa") of the [Independent Institute](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independent_Institute "Independent Institute") has hypothesized that Guevara's contemporary followers "delude themselves by clinging to a myth", describing Guevara as a "Marxist [Puritan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Puritans "Puritans")" who employed his rigid power to suppress dissent, while also operating as a "cold-blooded killing machine".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_172-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-ReferenceB-172">[172]</a></sup> Llosa also accuses Guevara's "fanatical disposition" as being the linchpin of the "Sovietization" of the Cuban revolution, speculating that he possessed a "total subordination of reality to blind ideological orthodoxy".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_172-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-ReferenceB-172">[172]</a></sup> On a macro-level, [Hoover Institution](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hoover_Institution "Hoover Institution") research fellow [William Ratliff](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Ratliff "William Ratliff") regards Guevara more as a creation of his historical environment, referring to him as a "fearless" and "head-strong Messiah-like figure", who was the product of a [martyr](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martyr "Martyr")\-enamored [Latin American culture](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_American_culture "Latin American culture") which "inclined people to seek out and follow [paternalistic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paternalism "Paternalism") miracle workers".<sup id="cite_ref-Ratliff07_314-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Ratliff07-314">[314]</a></sup> Ratliff further speculates that the economic conditions in the region suited Guevara's commitment to "bring justice to the downtrodden by crushing centuries-old tyrannies"; describing Latin America as being plagued by what [Moisés Naím](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mois%C3%A9s_Na%C3%ADm "Moisés Naím") referred to as the "legendary malignancies" of inequality, poverty, dysfunctional politics and malfunctioning institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-Ratliff07_314-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Ratliff07-314">[314]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SculptureCheGuevaraCuba.jpg)

[Plaza de la Revolución](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plaza_de_la_Revoluci%C3%B3n "Plaza de la Revolución"), in Havana, Cuba. Aside the Ministry of the Interior building where Guevara once worked is a 5-story steel outline of his face. Under the image is Guevara's motto, the Spanish phrase: _"Hasta la Victoria Siempre"_ (English: Until Victory, always).

In a mixed assessment, British historian [Hugh Thomas](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hugh_Thomas,_Baron_Thomas_of_Swynnerton "Hugh Thomas, Baron Thomas of Swynnerton") opined that Guevara was a "brave, sincere and determined man who was also obstinate, narrow, and dogmatic".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKellner1989106_315-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKellner1989106-315">[315]</a></sup> At the end of his life, according to Thomas, "he seems to have become convinced of the virtues of violence for its own sake", while "his influence over Castro for good or evil" grew after his death, as Fidel took up many of his views.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKellner1989106_315-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKellner1989106-315">[315]</a></sup> Similarly, the Cuban-American sociologist [Samuel Farber](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samuel_Farber "Samuel Farber") lauds Che Guevara as "an honest and committed revolutionary", but also criticizes the fact that "he never embraced socialism in its most democratic essence".<sup id="cite_ref-316"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-316">[316]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Guevara remains a national hero in Cuba, where his image adorns the 3 [peso](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuban_peso "Cuban peso") banknote and school children begin each morning by pledging "We will be like Che."<sup id="cite_ref-317"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-317">[317]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-318"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-318">[318]</a></sup> In his homeland of Argentina, where high schools bear his name,<sup id="cite_ref-319"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-319">[319]</a></sup> numerous Che museums dot the country and in 2008 a 3.5-metre (12 ft) bronze statue of him was unveiled in the city of his birth, Rosario.<sup id="cite_ref-320"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-320">[320]</a></sup> Guevara has been [sanctified](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanctification "Sanctification") by some Bolivian campesinos<sup id="cite_ref-Tobar2004_321-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-Tobar2004-321">[321]</a></sup> as "[Saint Ernesto](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara_in_popular_culture#In_religion "Che Guevara in popular culture")", who pray to him for assistance.<sup id="cite_ref-322"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup> In contrast, Guevara remains a hated figure amongst many in the [Cuban exile](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuban_exile "Cuban exile") and [Cuban American](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuban_Americans "Cuban Americans") community of the United States, who view him as "the butcher of [La Cabaña](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/La_Caba%C3%B1a "La Cabaña")".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECasey2009235,_325_323-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-FOOTNOTECasey2009235,_325-323">[323]</a></sup> Despite this polarized status, a high-contrast [monochrome](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monochrome "Monochrome") graphic of [Che's face](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guerrillero_Heroico "Guerrillero Heroico"), created in 1968 by Irish artist [Jim Fitzpatrick](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jim_Fitzpatrick_(artist) "Jim Fitzpatrick (artist)"), became a universally [merchandized](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Merchandising "Merchandising") and objectified image,<sup id="cite_ref-324"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-325"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup> found on an endless array of items, including T-shirts, hats, posters, tattoos, and bikinis,<sup id="cite_ref-326"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-326">[326]</a></sup> contributing to the [consumer culture](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consumer_capitalism "Consumer capitalism") Guevara despised. Yet, he still remains a transcendent figure both in specifically political contexts<sup id="cite_ref-327"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup> and as a wide-ranging popular icon of youthful rebellion.<sup id="cite_ref-myth_310-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-myth-310">[310]</a></sup>

### International honors

Guevara received several honors of state during his life.

-   **1960**: Knight Grand Cross of the [Order of the White Lion](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_of_the_White_Lion "Order of the White Lion")<sup id="cite_ref-328"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-328">[328]</a></sup>
-   **1961**: Knight Grand Cross of the [Order of the Southern Cross](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_of_the_Southern_Cross "Order of the Southern Cross")<sup id="cite_ref-329"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_note-329">[329]</a></sup>

-   Guevara interviewed in [Paris](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paris,_France "Paris, France") and speaking French in 1964, (4:47), English subtitles, interviewed by Jean Dumur, [video clip](https://www.youtube.com/watch_popup?v=128waCCK40I)<sup><span>[<i><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title="&nbsp;Dead link tagged February 2024">dead link</span></a></i>]</span></sup>

-   Guevara speaking about labor, (0:28), English subtitles, from _El Che: Investigating a Legend_ – Kultur Video 2001, [video clip](https://www.youtube.com/watch_popup?v=xh-MB_NDr-o)
-   Guevara speaking about the [Bay of Pigs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion "Bay of Pigs Invasion"), (0:17), English subtitles, from _El Che: Investigating a Legend_ – Kultur Video 2001, [video clip](https://www.youtube.com/watch_popup?v=OMA7Jv1RWIA)
-   Guevara speaking against [imperialism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imperialism "Imperialism"), (1:20), English subtitles, from _El Che: Investigating a Legend_ – Kultur Video 2001, [video clip](https://www.youtube.com/watch_popup?v=wdo6FwAPyng)
-   Guevara visiting Algeria in 1963 and giving a speech in French, from the Algerian Cinema Archive, [video clip](https://www.youtube.com/embed/kWNTvvoAja0)

-   Guevara interviewed on ABC's _[Issues and Answers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Issues_and_Answers "Issues and Answers")_, (22:27), English translation, narrated by [Lisa Howard](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lisa_Howard_(reporter) "Lisa Howard (reporter)"), 24 March 1964, [audio clip](https://www.youtube.com/embed/I3wAQG6HUGQ)

-   _Che Guevara: Radical Writings on Guerrilla Warfare, Politics and Revolution_,   Filiquarian Publishing, 2006, [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [1599869993](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/1599869993 "Special:BookSources/1599869993")

-   _Che Guevara Speaks: Selected Speeches and Writings_,   Pathfinder Press (NY), 1980, [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [0873486021](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/0873486021 "Special:BookSources/0873486021")
-   _Che Guevara Talks to Young People_,   Pathfinder, 2000, [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [087348911X](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/087348911X "Special:BookSources/087348911X")
-   _Che: The Diaries of Ernesto Che Guevara_,   Ocean Press (AU), 2008, [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [1920888934](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/1920888934 "Special:BookSources/1920888934")
-   _Colonialism is Doomed_,   Ministry of External Relations: Republic of Cuba, 1964, [ASIN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazon_Standard_Identification_Number "Amazon Standard Identification Number") [B0010AAN1K](https://www.amazon.com/dp/B0010AAN1K)
-   _Congo Diary: The Story of Che Guevara's "Lost" Year in Africa_   Ocean Press, 2011, [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0980429299](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0980429299 "Special:BookSources/978-0980429299")

## References

1.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-birthdate_1-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-birthdate_1-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-birthdate_1-2) The date of birth recorded on [his birth certificate](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/Ernesto_Guevara_Acta_de_Nacimiento.jpg) was 14 June 1928, although one tertiary source, (Julia Constenla, quoted by [Jon Lee Anderson](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jon_Lee_Anderson "Jon Lee Anderson")), asserts that he was actually born on 14 May of that year. Constenla alleges that she was told by Che's mother, Celia de la Serna, that she was already pregnant when she and Ernesto Guevara Lynch were married and that the date on the birth certificate of their son was forged to make it appear that he was born a month later than the actual date to avoid scandal. ([Anderson 1997](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#CITEREFAnderson1997), pp. 3, 769.)
2.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-2 "Jump up")** Partido Unido de la Revolución Socialista de Cuba, a.k.a. PURSC.
3.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-3 "Jump up")** [How to pronounce Che Guevara](http://www.forvo.com/word/che_guevara#es) – Forvo features various sound clips of international Spanish speakers enunciating his name.
4.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-FOOTNOTECasey2009128_4-0 "Jump up")** [Casey 2009](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#CITEREFCasey2009), p. 128.
5.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-RevMedicine_5-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-RevMedicine_5-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-RevMedicine_5-2) [On Revolutionary Medicine](https://www.marxists.org/archive/guevara/1960/08/19.htm) Speech by Che Guevara to the Cuban Militia on 19 August 1960. "Because of the circumstances in which I traveled, first as a student and later as a doctor, I came into close contact with poverty, hunger and disease; with the inability to treat a child because of lack of money; with the stupefaction provoked by the continual hunger and punishment, to the point that a father can accept the loss of a son as an unimportant accident, as occurs often in the downtrodden classes of our American homeland. And I began to realize at that time that there were things that were almost as important to me as becoming famous or making a significant contribution to medical science: I wanted to help those people."

11.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson1997526%E2%80%93530_11-0 "Jump up")** [Anderson 1997](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#CITEREFAnderson1997), pp. 526–530.
12.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-12 "Jump up")** ["On Development"](https://www.marxists.org/archive/guevara/1964/03/25.htm) Speech delivered by Che Guevara at the plenary session of the United Nations Conference on Trade and Development in Geneva, Switzerland on 25 March 1964. "The inflow of capital from the developed countries is the prerequisite for the establishment of economic dependence. This inflow takes various forms: loans granted on onerous terms; investments that place a given country in the power of the investors; almost total technological subordination of the dependent country to the developed country; control of a country's foreign trade by the big international monopolies; and in extreme cases, the use of force as an economic weapon in support of the other forms of exploitation."
13.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-AfroAsian1965_13-0 "Jump up")** ["At the Afro-Asian Conference in Algeria - A speech by Che Guevara to the Second Economic Seminar of Afro-Asian Solidarity in Algiers, Algeria"](https://www.marxists.org/archive/guevara/1965/02/24.htm). 24 February 1965 – via [Marxists Internet Archive](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marxists_Internet_Archive "Marxists Internet Archive"). The struggle against imperialism, for liberation from colonial or neocolonial shackles, which is being carried out by means of political weapons, arms, or a combination of the two, is not separate from the struggle against backwardness and poverty. Both are stages on the same road leading toward the creation of a new society of justice and plenty. ... Ever since monopoly capital took over the world, it has kept the greater part of humanity in poverty, dividing all the profits among the group of the most powerful countries. The standard of living in those countries is based on the extreme poverty of our countries. To raise the living standards of the underdeveloped nations, therefore, we must fight against imperialism. ... The practice of proletarian internationalism is not only a duty for the peoples struggling for a better future, it is also an inescapable necessity.
14.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-14 "Jump up")** Guevara was coordinating with African liberation movements in exile such as the [MPLA](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/MPLA "MPLA") in Angola and [MNR](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Movement_of_the_Revolution "National Movement of the Revolution") in Congo-Brazzaville, while stating that Africa represented one of "the more important fields of struggle against all forms of exploitation existing in the world". Guevara then envisioned crafting an alliance with African leaders such as Ahmed Ben Bella in Algeria, Sékou Touré in Guinea, Kwame Nkrumah in Ghana, Julius Nyerere in Tanzania, and Gamal Abdel Nasser in Egypt, to foster a global dimension to his ensuing continental revolution in Latin America. See [Anderson 1997](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#CITEREFAnderson1997), pp. 576, 584.
15.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-15 "Jump up")** [Ryan 1998](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#refRyan1998), p. 4.
16.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-16 "Jump up")** Footnote for _[Socialism and man in Cuba](https://www.marxists.org/archive/guevara/1965/03/man-socialism.htm)_ (1965): "Che argued that the full liberation of humankind is reached when work becomes a social duty carried out with complete satisfaction and sustained by a value system that contributes to the realization of conscious action in performing tasks. This could only be achieved by systematic education, acquired by passing through various stages in which collective action is increased. Che recognized that this to be difficult and time-consuming. In his desire to speed up this process, however, he developed methods of mobilizing people, bringing together their collective and individual interests. Among the most significant of these instruments were moral and material incentives, while deepening consciousness as a way of developing toward socialism. See Che's speeches: _Homage to Emulation Prize Winners_ (1962) and _A New Attitude to Work_ (1964)."
17.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-17 "Jump up")** [Dorfman 1999](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#refDorfman1999).
18.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-18 "Jump up")** Maryland Institute of Art, referenced at [BBC News 26 May 2001](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#CITEREFBBCNews2001b).
19.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-19 "Jump up")** In Spanish a person may carry the surname of his or her father as well as that of his or her mother, albeit in that order. Some people carry both, others only that of their father. In Guevara's case, many people of Irish descent will add "Lynch" to emphasize his Irish relations. Others will add "de la Serna" to give respect to Guevara's mother.
20.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuevara_Lynch2007i_20-0 "Jump up")** [Guevara Lynch 2007](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#CITEREFGuevara_Lynch2007), p. i: "The father of Che Guevara, Ernesto Guevara Lynch was born in Argentina in 1900 of Irish and Basque origin."

22.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-22 "Jump up")** Che's last name _Guevara_ derives from the [Castilianized](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Castilian_Spanish "Castilian Spanish") form of the [Basque](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basque_language "Basque language") _[Gebara](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guevara,_Spain "Guevara, Spain")_, a [habitational name](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitational_name "Habitational name") from the province of [Álava](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%81lava "Álava"), while his grandmother, Ana Lynch, was a descendant of [Patrick Lynch](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Patrick_Lynch_(Argentina) "Patrick Lynch (Argentina)"), who emigrated from [County Galway](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/County_Galway,_Ireland "County Galway, Ireland"), Ireland in the 1740s.

53.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-53 "Jump up")** A copy of Guevara's University transcripts showing conferral of his medical diploma can be found on p. 75 of _Becoming Che: Guevara's Second and Final Trip through Latin America_, by Carlos 'Calica' Ferrer (Translated from the Spanish by Sarah L. Smith), Marea Editorial, 2006, [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9871307071](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/9871307071 "Special:BookSources/9871307071"). Ferrer was a longtime childhood friend of Che, and when Guevara passed the last of his 12 exams in 1953, he gave Ferrer, who had been telling Guevara that he would never finish, a copy, showing that he had finally completed his studies.

133.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-133 "Jump up")** Different sources cite differing numbers of executions attributable to Guevara, with some of the discrepancy resulting from the question of which deaths to attribute directly to Guevara and which to the regime as a whole. [Anderson 1997](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#CITEREFAnderson1997) gives the number specifically at La Cabaña prison as 55 (p. 387.), while also stating that "several hundred people were officially tried and executed across Cuba" as a whole (p. 387). ([Castañeda 1998](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#refCasta%C3%B1eda1998)) notes that historians differ on the total number killed, with different studies placing it as anywhere from 200 to 700 nationwide (p. 143), although he notes that "after a certain date most of the executions occurred outside of Che's jurisdiction" (p. 143). These numbers are supported by the opposition-based [_Free Society Project / Cuba Archive_](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#refFreeSoc), which gives the figure as 144 executions ordered by Guevara across Cuba in three years (1957–1959) and 105 "victims" specifically at La Cabaña, which according to them were all "carried out without due process of law". Of further note, much of the discrepancy in the estimates between 55 versus 105 executed at La Cabaña revolves around whether to include instances where Guevara had denied an appeal and signed off on a death warrant, but where the sentence was carried out while he traveled overseas from 4 June to 8 September, or after he relinquished his command of the fortress on 12 June 1959.

273.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#cite_ref-273 "Jump up")** After the Cuban revolution, seeing that Guevara had no watch, his friend Oscarito Fernández Mell gave him his own gold watch. Sometime later, Che handed him a piece of paper; a receipt from the National Bank declaring that Mell had "donated" his gold wristband to Cuba's gold reserve. Guevara was still wearing his watch, but it now had a leather wristband ([Anderson 1997](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Che_Guevara#CITEREFAnderson1997), p. 503).
